B.8. Cosmology
B.8.1. Weak lensing cosmology analysis / cosmic shear

Contributors:
(arunkannawadi@astro.princeton.edu), Andresa Campos (acampos@cmu.edu)

Rachel Mandelbaum (rmandelb@andrew.cmu.edu), Arun Kannawadi

B.8.1.1. Abstract ‚ÄîWe present the Ô¨Årst cosmological analysis of the weak lensing shear-shear
correlation functions, which trace the evolution of large-scale structure from intermediate
to low redshift. With the Ô¨Årst year of LSST data, we can already constrain the amplitude of
matter Ô¨Çuctuations to greater precision than precursor surveys such as Kilo-Degree Survey
(KiDS), DES, and HSC, reaching ~1% precision when considering statistical and systematic
uncertainty. This improved result is due to the increased area of LSST, and the fact that
the Ô¨Årst year of data is comparable in depth to DES and deeper than KiDS. This work will
rely on analysis of the catalogs produced by the LSST Science Pipelines down to detections
with ~10ùúé signiÔ¨Åcance. This result is suÔ¨Éciently precise to permit a robust test of a Œõ Cold
Dark Matter; cosmological model (LCDM) by comparing with the amplitude of Ô¨Çuctuations
from the CMB, which current analyses suggest may be higher than that from weak lensing
(implying some uncontrolled systematic or a failure of the LCDM model). Together with
the CMB data, we can go beyond the LCDM model and provide competitive constraints on
the equation of state of dark energy.

B.8.1.2. Science Objectives ‚ÄîMilestones to this result include deÔ¨Åning samples that pass
basic null tests, validating that the shear estimates meet the needs for the science, and
estimating the ensemble redshift distribution N(z) for the samples selected based on photo-
z. For the last step, we do not necessarily need very precise photo-z (though that would
improve the localization of the selected redshift bins and improve the constraint on w) but
we do need a very precise understanding of the N(z) for the ensemble. At this stage, we
anticipate that photo-z calibration and the impact of blending on shear and photo-z are likely
to be the key limited systematics.

B.8.1.3. Challenges (what makes it hard) ‚ÄîComputational challenges related to image anal-
ysis, shear calibration, and cosmological likelihood analysis tend to compete with building
suÔ¨Écient models for systematics as the biggest challenge.

Broadly, there are two kinds of systematics: modeling systematics that are mostly astro-
physical in nature and systematics in our data. Understanding astrophysical systematics
that arise from our lack of understanding of baryonic physics and galaxy formation relies
on information beyond the LSST data to mitigate their impact on cosmological parameter
constraints. While some amount of self-calibration may be possible, we need to improve
external (beyond LSST) measurements and simulations that give us direct constraints on
baryonic feedback, intrinsic alignment etc.

Key systematics of both types are listed below:

‚Ä¢ intrinsic alignments of galaxy shapes (IA): At LSST precision, modeling the intrinsic
alignments of galaxy shapes with the large-scale density Ô¨Åeld is necessary. They are

Data to Software to Science

157

one of the major drivers of the overall uncertainty in current cosmic-shear analyses.
We will need tighter priors from external measurements and/or from hydrodynamical
simulations. Self-calibration of IA parameters from the observations is also a possible
approach.

‚Ä¢ Blending: At LSST depths, a substantial fraction of the galaxies are blended. Nour-
bakhsh et al. (2021) estimates 12% of LSST galaxies would constitute undetected
blends, i.e., two overlapping galaxies In general, they could have vastly diÔ¨Äerent
redshifts which necessitates a deblending algorithm that can apportion the Ô¨Çux to the
diÔ¨Äerent galaxies. This is currently done using the SCARLET algorithm (Melchior
et al. 2018), which uses multiband images to model out the neighboring galaxies.
Synthetic source injection tools that are developed jointly between DM and DESC
can be used to estimate the sensitivity of the measurement to the amount of blending
present. On longer time scales, pixel-level joint processing of Euclid+Rubin images
to create Euclid+Rubin DDPs will provide an even better handle on deblending,
especially undetected blends.

‚Ä¢ PSF estimation: Zhang et al. (2022) estimates the bias in the cosmological parameters
from errors in higher-order moments. They Ô¨Ånd that errors from the current version
of the state-of-the-art algorithm PiÔ¨Ä (now default in Rubin Science Pipelines) are
comparable with PSFEx and that they introduce biases that are 0.2ùúé in the structure
growth parameter ùëÜ8 in LSST Y1 analysis.

‚Ä¢ Shear calibration: Even in the absence of any blending, source detection algorithms
may preferentially detect sources that are sensitive to ellipticities/shape of the source.
This introduces a detection or selection bias in the sample (Kannawadi et al. 2019).
This could be corrected for by introducing a detection step in the shear measurement,
which is essentially what metadetection achieves (Sheldon et al. 2020). The require-
ment on the shear multiplicative bias is at the level of 10‚àí3. While shear measurement
algorithms such as metadetection reach this level in simulations with a number of
realistic eÔ¨Äects (E. Sheldon, internal communication), this could go beyond the re-
quirements if various uncorrected systematic eÔ¨Äects add up coherently. Can we get
shear measurement codes to the stage where they do not need any image simulations
for calibration (only validation)? It would be an ever-increasing burden to calibrate
shear if calibration simulations are a requirement.

‚Ä¢ Photo-z calibration: Blending impacts the ability to precisely estimate ensemble red-
shift distributions, which can ultimately cause a bias in the cosmological parameters,
as shown in Nourbakhsh et al. (2021). Although we expect to have a diÔ¨Äerent de-
blending methodology from the one in the above paper, it shows the importance of
being able to perform deblending accurately.

‚Ä¢ Sociological systematic: True blinding is diÔ¨Écult to achieve since the Rubin data
products are available to the entire data rights community. DESC products can be
blinded, but that would necessarily imply that Rubin data products cannot be used
oÔ¨Ä-the-shelf for cosmological analysis by DESC.

158

These analyses will be done with a subset of the LSST data releases. The analyses will be
mostly constrained by the major validation eÔ¨Äort that is required before getting the results
published, which has meant that most precursor surveys do not carry out cosmological shear
measurements with each new data release.

B.8.1.4. Existing Tools ‚Äî

‚Ä¢ The following tools already exist today for this analysis

‚Äì RAIL+qp for getting a p(z) for each object
‚Äì Metadetection algorithm (descwl_coadd, mdet-shear-sims. . . )
‚Äì Code from Pedersen et al. on self-calibrating IA
‚Äì Synthetic Source Injection (DM/DESC)
‚Äì Theoretical modelling (e.g., CCL, HMCode/HaloÔ¨Åt), correlation functions (e.g.,

TreeCorr, NaMaster, TXPipe), Covariances, Inference (e.g., CosmoSIS)

‚Ä¢ The following functionality is still missing from these tools:

‚Äì Storing the p(z) for each object from diÔ¨Äerent colors could be memory-intensive.
We need either an uber-fast algorithm that can take in colors and reference
catalogs and spit out p(z) on-demand, or Ô¨Ånd an eÔ¨Äective compression technique
to store them.

‚Äì Current Synthetic Source Injection (SSI) software is not equipped to inject
sources onto coadds, since the ability to inject a source and additional noise that
is consistent with the source is not yet present. Developing this ability could
save a lot of processing time.

B.8.1.5. Running on LSST Datasets (for the Ô¨Årst 2 years) ‚ÄîWe will use the following data
products:

‚Ä¢ Data release catalogs for the Wide Fast Deep (WFD) survey - we will use essentially

all galaxies down to some relatively low signiÔ¨Åcance (~10-sigma).

‚Ä¢ Some calibration and systematics tests will use the DDFs.
‚Ä¢ External spectroscopic catalogs to validate photometric redshifts.
‚Ä¢ We will need some image simulations (beyond DESC DC2) that mock-up the cell-
based coaddition to validate the shears, and cross-matching and cross-correlation
against spectroscopic surveys to validate the photo-z and ensemble N(z) estimates.
‚Ä¢ Euclid+Rubin DDPs for deblending (Schuhmann et al. 2019 predict mean shrinkage
in ellipticity error bars by more than a factor of 2 when LSST is combined with
Euclid)

B.8.1.6. Precursor data sets ‚Äî

‚Ä¢ DES, HSC, and KiDS all have public data releases that could be used for precursor
In some cases, the catalogs are most accessible while reanalyzing the

analyses.
images would be a substantial challenge despite data being made public.

Data to Software to Science

159

‚Ä¢ Reference spectroscopic samples (deep ones for direct calibration and wide ones from

e.g. DESI for cross-correlation) will be important for the N(z) validation.

B.8.1.7. Analysis WorkÔ¨Çow ‚ÄîAssuming the existence of catalogs with galaxy shape or shear
measurements and with photometric redshifts, the analysis workÔ¨Çow is as follows:

1. Apply selection criteria to the catalogs following some pre-deÔ¨Åned process to mitigate

selection biases (potentially using the metadetection technique).

2. Apply any shear calibration or other steps.
3. Divide the sample into tomographic (redshift) bins based on the photometric redshift

estimates.

4. For each tomographic bin, apply a method for inferring the ensemble redshift dis-
tribution, N(z), based on the photometric redshifts and/or other information such as
clustering with a reference spectroscopic sample.

5. Measure various null tests designed to detect the impact of systematic biases; de-

pending on the results, may need to iterate on the above steps.

6. Measure shear correlation functions using pairs of tomographic bins ùëñ and ùëó.
7. Produce a covariance matrix using some method potentially based on numerical

integration of analytic expressions or based on mock catalogs.

8. Carry out a likelihood analysis for the cosmological parameters based on the measured
data vectors and covariance matrices, including models for astrophysical and other
systematics.

B.8.1.8. Software Capabilities Needed ‚ÄîNeeded capabilities include the following:

‚Ä¢ Survey property maps (e.g., skyproj with healsparse12)
‚Ä¢ Rubin calibrated exposures and coadds
‚Ä¢ Estimators for ensemble redshift distributions

B.8.1.9. References ‚ÄîPrecursor survey analyses:

‚Ä¢ KiDS-1000: Asgari et al. (2021)
‚Ä¢ DES-Y3: Amon et al. (2022)
‚Ä¢ HSC-Y1: Hamana et al. (2020)

B.8.2. Probabilistic Type Ia supernova cosmology analysis

Contributors:
delb@andrew.cmu.edu)

Alex Malz

(aimalz@nyu.edu), Rachel Mandelbaum (rman-

B.8.2.1. Abstract ‚ÄîOur scientiÔ¨Åc objective is to constrain the expansion history and cosmo-
logical parameters using photometric Type Ia supernovae from the Ô¨Årst ~year of LSST data,
in combination with precursor distance ladder measurements. In particular, we would like to
go beyond the traditional analysis methods, by self-consistently incorporating probabilistic
information as follows:

‚Ä¢ ClassiÔ¨Åcation probabilities (e.g., with BEAMS)
‚Ä¢ Host identiÔ¨Åcation probabilities (e.g., with zBEAMS)
‚Ä¢ Redshift probabilities ‚Äì e.g., with Supernova Cosmology Inference with Probabilistic

Photometric Redshifts (SCIPPR)

‚Ä¢ Selection function
‚Ä¢ Debiasing on the (probabilistic) absolute magnitude.

The probabilistic approach will permit the use of a larger sample in early LSST data
compared to a non-probabilistic approach that requires secure follow-up in early LSST data.

B.8.2.2. Science Objectives ‚ÄîThe key steps in the analysis:

‚Ä¢ Alert broker provides classiÔ¨Åcation probabilities and redshift posteriors for possible

hosts for potential supernovae.

‚Äì If using early (Ô¨Årst year) data, it will be challenging to understand the selection
functions which come with light curves correctly classiÔ¨Åed as Type Ia SNe.
‚Äì We run our own classiÔ¨Åcation using additional points in the light curve after the

initial detection of the supernovae.

‚Ä¢ We apply additional selection criteria to identify ones that can be used for science,
and potentially get new host probabilities and redshift posteriors (but still need the
originals because they go into the selection function).

‚Ä¢ Light curve Ô¨Åtting with population-level debiasing function.
‚Ä¢ Hierarchical inference of cosmological parameters factoring in probabilistic infor-
mation for hosts, classiÔ¨Åcation, and redshift (though none of the existing algorithms
currently work at scale).

B.8.2.3. Challenges (what makes it hard) ‚ÄîThe key technical limitation is that most of the
steps in this analysis do not run at scale, while it‚Äôs not clear how to do selection function
quantiÔ¨Åcation from the level of alerts, so algorithm development needs a lot of thought.

Early light curve Ô¨Åts will be particularly impacted by sparsity. Lack of redshifts from
Rubin in the Ô¨Årst year will be an issue - the brokers will get them from diÔ¨Äerent sources and
they will deÔ¨Ånitely diÔ¨Äer from DESC photo-z.

Data to Software to Science

161

B.8.2.4. Running on LSST Datasets (for the Ô¨Årst 2 years) ‚ÄîThe analysis will use:

‚Ä¢ The alert stream.
‚Ä¢ DESC value-added photo-z catalogs.

B.8.2.5. Precursor data sets ‚ÄîPLAsTiCC and ELAsTiCC data challenges can be used as a
test-bed, but photo-z are not self-consistently generated with the light curves, especially for
PLAsTiCC. ELAsTiCC results could potentially be used to determine the selection function
(validate a method for doing so), but the sample may not be large enough. A preliminary
study using Pan-STARRS Medium Deep Survey data has already been conducted (see Jones
et al. 2018a).

B.8.2.6. Analysis WorkÔ¨Çow ‚ÄîRunning the analysis requires us to run these steps:

‚Ä¢ Classify lightcurves using additional points after the initial detection.
‚Ä¢ Quantify selection function - limited by basic algorithmic questions.
‚Ä¢ Probabilistic lightcurve and absolute magnitude Ô¨Åtting.
‚Ä¢ Use redshift uncertainties and classiÔ¨Åcation probabilities for cosmology (BEAMS -
does not work at scale), host identiÔ¨Åcation probabilities (zBEAMS - simple assump-
tions, does not work at scale), redshift probabilities (scippr - open source, does not
work at scale)

‚Ä¢ Hierarchical inference at scale, combining all of the above components in a self-

consistent manner.

B.8.2.7. Software Capabilities Needed ‚ÄîThe needed software capabilities boil down to in-
tegrating and scaling up existing probabilistic approaches. There are also visualization
needs for probabilistic data products connected to intuitive traditional plots, which could be
implemented in the RSP.

B.8.2.8. References for Further Reading ‚Äî

‚Ä¢ Bayesian Estimation with multiple Species (BEAMS): Kunz et al. (2007)
‚Ä¢ zBEAMS (BEAMS plus host identiÔ¨Åcation uncertainty)- Roberts et al. (2017)
‚Ä¢ Supernova Cosmology Inference with Probabilistic Photometric Redshifts (SCIPPR):
open source code for incorporating probabilistic classiÔ¨Åcation and redshift to cosmol-
ogy inference, does not yet work at scale - Peters et al. (2018); https://github.com/
aimalz/scippr

‚Ä¢ Photometric LSST Astronomical Time-series ClassiÔ¨Åcation Challenge (PLAsTiCC):

The PLAsTiCC team et al. (2018); Hlo≈æek et al. (2020)

B.8.3. Optimal spectroscopic follow-up algorithms for Type Ia supernova cosmology

Contributors:
(emille.ishida@clermont.in2p3.fr), Bruno Quint

Alex Malz

(aimalz@nyu.edu),

Emille

E. O.

Ishida

B.8.3.1.
Abstract ‚ÄîThe Recommendation System for Spectroscopic Follow-up
(RESSPECT) project is a COIN-DESC joint endeavour which aims to enable the con-
struction of optimized training samples for LSST photometric SN cosmology. It takes into
account a realistic description of the astronomical data environment and available follow-up
resources. Early LSST data can be used to validate the ReSSpect pipeline, ensuring the
system is able to scale to LSST requirements and helping deÔ¨Åne which categories of tran-
sients have the potential to boost performance of machine learning classiÔ¨Åers ‚Äì if included
in the training sample. The system requires daily updates on its data sets with every newly
observed photometric point and the entire pipeline should be daily run in order to identify
the optimal candidates for the subsequent night. As a consequence, signiÔ¨Åcant computing
resources are required to daily process all updated data which survives selection cuts. By
construction, half of the objects selected for follow-up will be composed of SNIa and the
other half will contain transients which are easily mistaken with SNIa by ML classiÔ¨Åers.
The Ô¨Ånal queried spectroscopic sample, when used as a training sample, will enable opti-
mum results from any supervised learning classiÔ¨Åer - thus ensuring exploitation of purely
photometric LSST light curves for SN cosmology.

B.8.3.2. Science Objectives ‚ÄîCosmological analysis with LSST‚Äôs photometric supernova
sample will be essential because the spectroscopically conÔ¨Årmed (and even spectroscopi-
cally conÔ¨Årmable) sample will not be suÔ¨Éciently large to improve upon the current status
In order to fully take advantadge of the information contained in LSST data, it is
quo.
necessary to optimize the exploitation of purely photometric light curves.

Photometric supernova cosmology requires classiÔ¨Åcation of lightcurves, primarily done
by machine learning algorithms conditioned on a training set. Currently, the only possible
available option is to train on simulations or on the available spectroscopic samples. Al-
though encouraging results have been obtained by using simulations as training, this method
inevitably biases the results towards the already known ‚Äì and modelled ‚Äì light curve features
present in templates. Alternatively, currently available spectroscopic samples are highly
biased towards SNIa-like events, which leads to sub-optimal performance from ML classi-
Ô¨Åers. From the algorithm point of view, an ideal training sample should enclose examples
not only of the class of interest, but also on all possible objects which can be mistaken by it.
Our Ô¨Ånal goal is to enable purely photometric supernova cosmology by using RESSPECT
to construct informative training samples for ML classiÔ¨Åers. In parallel, the construction
of this highly informative sample will also enable further development in the modelling of
astrophysical transients - in particular SN events which lie in the boundary of the Ia class.

B.8.3.3. Challenges (what makes it hard) ‚Äî

Data to Software to Science

163

‚Ä¢ Feature extraction: the pipeline requires that all incoming data are represented as a
homogeneous rectangular matrix. It currently transforms all data into features using
a parametric Ô¨Åt. This procedure needs to be repeated every time a new photometric
point is observed. This process is slow and scales fast with the number of objects
which needs to be processed at each night.

‚Ä¢ Retraining: The active learning loop requires the model to be retrained at each night
after the data has been updated. Moreover, in order to use the cosmology metric to
disentangle equally valuable batches of potential candidates, the training needs to be
repeated multiple times for each batch. This procedure needs to be done before the
subsequent observation night. As a consequence, the classiÔ¨Åer inside the AL loop
needs to be cheap and straight forward to train.

‚Ä¢ Cosmology Ô¨Åt:

the cosmology metric calculation is quite slow and is based on a
simplistic model because anything more sophisticated would be even slower. Never-
theless, it still requires a lot of computational time to enable the cost calculation for
a series of multiple batches.

‚Ä¢ Updated substream of alerts: after the retraining is done, the updated ML model
needs to be used to Ô¨Ålter the original data stream (provided via one of the community
brokers). This will require interaction between the pipeline and the broker interface,
since the entire process should be automatized.

‚Ä¢ Communication with follow-up facilities: once the list of candidates is identiÔ¨Åed,
we need to automatically distribute the list to the available spectroscopic follow-up
facilities. And once the labels are obtained they need to be included in the training.
Ideally these processes would be automatic.

‚Ä¢ Timing constraint: results cannot be put to use right away. What we learn from doing
this will build the training set we use for photometric SN cosmology in early LSST
data and inform follow-up strategies after year 1.

B.8.3.4. Running on LSST Datasets (for the Ô¨Årst 2 years) ‚ÄîThe analysis will use:

‚Ä¢ The Ô¨Åltered alert stream (removing artifacts and already known transients identiÔ¨Åed

via cross-match with publicly available catalogs)

‚Ä¢ Optionally utilize DESC value-added photo-z catalogs
‚Ä¢ Initial training requires a minimum number of spectroscopically classiÔ¨Åed objects (5

Ias and 5 non-Ia would be enough)

‚Ä¢ Validation: requires set of complete light curves which could potentially be used for

cosmology (pass quality cuts, pending classiÔ¨Åcation)

‚Ä¢ Spectroscopic classiÔ¨Åcations for appointed candidates: candidates would be known
at each night and classiÔ¨Åcation will be acquired using external follow-up resources
and publicly available catalogs

B.8.3.5. Precursor data sets ‚ÄîOur initial tests show that it is not advisable to use precursor
data sets for training. Starting from scratch with LSST data allows the construction of a
tailored data set and avoid bias in classiÔ¨Åcation results.

164

Figure 7. WorkÔ¨Çow (Ô¨Ågure courtesy of Bruno Quint)

B.8.3.6. Analysis WorkÔ¨Çow ‚ÄîThe key steps in the analysis (see also Figure 7):

‚Ä¢ Construction full light curve (validation) and small spectroscopic conÔ¨Årmed (initial

training) samples from alerts

‚Ä¢ Apply feature extraction to the validation and initial training samples
‚Ä¢ At each day (active learning loop):

‚Äì Apply feature extraction to the pool and training sample including newly ob-

served epochs
‚Äì Train classiÔ¨Åer
‚Äì Classify test and pool sets (yields classiÔ¨Åcation probabilities)
‚Äì Identify candidates for spectroscopic follow-up
‚Äì For each candidate, for each possible class:

‚àó Add the candidate with presumed label to the training sample
‚àó Train the classiÔ¨Åer
‚àó Classify the test sample
‚àó Select photometrically classiÔ¨Åed SN Ia
‚àó Perform SALT2 Ô¨Åt (parametric SN lightcurve model) on photometric SN

Ia sample

‚àó Add bias correction
‚àó Infer cosmological parameters (currently using a simpliÔ¨Åed Bayesian model

due to computational expense)

‚àó Quantify cosmological impact for the new candidate in training set

Data to Software to Science

165

‚Äì Combine cosmology metric of candidates into batches
‚Äì Evaluate observation cost of each batch
‚Äì Choose batch of candidates optimizing the overall metric
‚Äì Stream candidates to spectroscopic follow-up facilities
‚Äì Add new available labels (candidates from previous nights) to training set

B.8.3.7. Software Capabilities Needed ‚ÄîWe have all the moving parts in terms of the software
described above, and the pipeline works end-to-end on catalog data. We need an interface
to the real alert stream as well as the spectroscopic follow-up facilities. We need software
engineering to scale-up the computation (speciÔ¨Åcally the classiÔ¨Åer retraining and cosmology
metric evaluation) and the computing resources to run the pipeline nightly.

B.8.4. Cross-correlation between LSST and CMB probes of gas physics

Contributors: Giulio Fabbian (FabbianG@cardiÔ¨Ä.ac.uk), Rachel Mandelbaum (rman-
delb@andrew.cmu.edu)

B.8.4.1. Abstract ‚ÄîDuring its journey towards us, the CMB interacts with the large-scale
structures of the universe (e.g. galaxy clusters, Ô¨Ålaments, voids) as they form. This happens
mainly through gravitational lensing of its photons and their inverse-Compton scattering
with electrons having large thermal or bulk velocities (thermal Sunyaev-Zeldovich eÔ¨Äect
(tSZ) and kinetic Sunyaev-Zeldovich eÔ¨Äect (kSZ)). Thus, the CMB is also a powerful
tracer of the matter distribution in the universe. The matter distribution can also be
probed surveying the distribution of galaxies at diÔ¨Äerent redshifts and frequencies. The
inhomogeneity of the matter distribution in the universe aÔ¨Äects galaxy observations and
CMB similarly, but, crucially, not exactly in the same way so that a signiÔ¨Åcant amount
of information can be extracted thus from their joint analysis and through their cross-
correlations. Cross-correlations will help to break degeneracies between observables and
to isolate diÔ¨Äerent physical eÔ¨Äects otherwise indistinguishable. The Simons Observatory
(SO) will play a major role in this endeavor, providing a deep CMB polarization-sensitive
survey covering about half the sky and the southern hemisphere starting in ~2023.

Multi-frequency observations of the CMB can be used to extract maps of the tSZ eÔ¨Äect
through its characteristic spectral distortion imprinted in the CMB, and to separate it
from galactic foregrounds and extragalactic emission in the IR band. The kSZ signature
is observed in maps of the CMB temperature anisotropies that cannot be easily isolated
without the help of an external tracer given that its SED is the same as the one of CMB
anisotropies. Alongside SO and on the same timescale, the next generation of galaxy
surveys, the European Space Agency‚Äôs Euclid mission and the Vera C. Rubin Observatory
LSST, will map the mass distribution in the recent universe over more than half the sky with
complementary experimental strategies. Given their full overlap, SO, LSST and Euclid will
provide ideal data sets for joint studies and cross-correlation analyses.

B.8.4.2. Science Objectives ‚Äî

‚Ä¢ The cross-correlation between LSST galaxy density map and tSZ Compton ùë¶-map
will give us insight on the bias-weighted pressure proÔ¨Åle and energy content of
electrons in halos.

‚Ä¢ The cross-correlation between galaxy density of LSST and the square of the CMB
temperature map (essentially a probe of the (cid:104)ùëáùëá ùõø(cid:105) bispectrum) is sensitive to both
the electron density and velocity Ô¨Åeld (and hence a good probe of baryonic feedback
processes).

‚Ä¢ A good knowledge of the galaxy sample can be used to investigate the variation of
both the physical processes above as a function of redshift and galaxy environment
or mass.

Data to Software to Science

167

‚Ä¢ The analysis will use data products provided by CMB experiments and LSST catalogs
with potentially augmented properties to allow for systematic tests of the sample
selection and or investigation of variations as a function of environment properties.
‚Ä¢ The accuracy of the characterization of the galaxy sample‚Äôs ensemble redshift dis-
tribution (in a statistical sense) and extragalactic and galactic foregrounds aÔ¨Äecting
CMB data sets (e.g. modulation of sample selection function by extinction of galaxy
dust) will be the major potential systematic factor. Additive systematics aÔ¨Äecting
only the CMB or the LSST data should not have a large impact in cross-correlation
studies.

‚Ä¢ Demonstrating analyses are currently being carried out with current generation of
CMB data sets and publicly available surveys (that so far have been mainly optical
spectroscopic surveys or IR photometric data, such as catalogs built from WISE data).

B.8.4.3. Challenges (what makes it hard) ‚Äî

‚Ä¢ ScientiÔ¨Åc challenge: The need for joint simulations to test the analysis methods.
‚Ä¢ Technical challenge: Some cross-correlation software used for current surveys might

not scale well to LSST data volume.

‚Ä¢ Data quality:

‚Äì Practical limit in resolution is imposed by CMB survey. Atacama Cosmology
Telescope, for CMB observations (ACT)/SO give ~arcmin resolution ùë¶ and ùëá
maps on potentially the full LSST footprint, SPT-3G will have lower noise but
will cover a smaller sky area, while Planck has lower angular resolution and
higher noise but potentially larger sky coverage. The details of the best data set
to be used depends on the choice of the observing strategy and timing of the
CMB experiments, i.e., which sky area will be covered (and delivered) Ô¨Årst.
‚Äì The need to have a reasonably well-understood redshift distribution for the
galaxy sample used to deÔ¨Åne the density Ô¨Åeld could be a practical issue when
deÔ¨Åning the LSST galaxy sample.

‚Ä¢ Timing issue: There is not a major urgency to this analysis, though carrying it
out earlier rather than later might inform strategy and analysis plans for Cosmic
Microwave Background Stage 4 (CMB-S4).

B.8.4.4. Running on LSST Datasets (for the Ô¨Årst 2 years) ‚ÄîWe‚Äôll use the photometric galaxy
sample derived from object catalogs from 1 year of data (DR2). It will be important to have
a set of well-characterized photo-z to deÔ¨Åne tomographic source samples for which we will
infer ensemble redshift distributions. As long as the sample is reasonably homogeneous
and inhomogeneities are well understood, the analysis can proceed.

We‚Äôll want a value-added galaxy catalog with additional characterization of the galaxy

SEDs, local density, selection function, etc.

We‚Äôll also need a CMB dataset with suÔ¨Écient resolution to carry out this analysis, such

as ACT, South Pole Telescope (SPT), SO, etc.

168

B.8.4.5. Precursor data sets ‚ÄîPhotometric catalogs from KiDS, DES, and/or HSC can be
used to develop this use case though they cover a smaller area than LSST, so DES might
be the only viable option. Overlap with a CMB dataset is a key practical consideration to
deÔ¨Åne the details of the analysis. Joint simulations including realistic evolution of the dark
matter and baryon distribution on both the galaxy sample as well as on the CMB probes
will likely be important for carrying out this science with LSST and SO, as these probes are
expected to be measured with high precision and statistical signiÔ¨Åcance.

B.8.4.6. Analysis WorkÔ¨Çow ‚Äî

‚Ä¢ Select galaxy catalog and split it in diÔ¨Äerent mass or tomographic bins according to

LSST redshift estimates.

‚Ä¢ Make galaxy overdensity maps and store them.
‚Ä¢ Compute and store cross-correlation statistics (usually angular power spectra) be-
tween galaxy overdensity maps and external CMB temperature and/or tSZ maps.
‚Ä¢ Compute or estimate covariance from jackknife resampling or simulation or analytical

approximations.

‚Ä¢ Perform robustness or consistency/null tests using data splits constructed with subsets
of the galaxy catalog or CMB products built from subset of the data depending on
which data splits are available from the CMB side.

‚Ä¢ Inference of relevant parameters.

B.8.4.7. Software Capabilities Needed ‚Äî

‚Ä¢ Query the LSST archive and make galaxy catalogs with various cuts and desired
properties, including division into tomographic samples (distinct redshift bins).
‚Ä¢ Map-making software, to construct maps of projected quantities such as galaxy

overdensity at a variety of resolutions

‚Ä¢ Ensemble redshift distribution inference for photometric samples.
‚Ä¢ Cross-power spectrum calculation that takes maps as inputs.
‚Ä¢ Theory predictions of 2-point statistics (through Core Cosmology Library, https:

//github.com/LSSTDESC/CCL (CCL) or similar software)

‚Ä¢ Monte Carlo Markov Chain (MCMC) sampler or likelihood-free inference code

[which would require fast simulations / new software development]

In some cases new methods or optimized software are needed but they are common needs
for other cosmology science cases (e.g., 3x2pt analysis). Joint simulations would be more
speciÔ¨Åc to this LSST+CMB science case.
Maps and power spectra points and covariances will be stored.

B.8.5. Self-consistent cosmological parameter constraints from galaxy clustering and

galaxy-galaxy lensing using the DESI Y1 LRG sample

Contributors: Kate Storey-Fisher (k.sf@nyu.edu), Francois Lanusse, Sam Schmidt, Arun
Kannawadi

B.8.5.1. Abstract ‚ÄîWe present the results of a galaxy clustering and galaxy-galaxy lensing
analysis using the Rubin LSST photometric sample and the DESI spectroscopic sample.
DESI is the current largest spectroscopic redshift survey, and is still ongoing. The two
surveys have an overlap region of at least ~4000 deg2, and could increase to as much as
6000 deg2 depending on footprint decisions (Lochner et al. 2022). We use the DESI LRG
sample, which contains ~8 million galaxies from 0.3 < z < 1, as the lenses. We model
galaxy bias, baryonic feedback, and intrinsic alignments, and the high precision of DESI
allows us to break degeneracies to better handle these systematics. We constrain Œ©ùëö to
ùëã ¬± ùëå and ùëÜ8 to ùëã ùëã ¬± ùëåùëå . This lensing analysis from combining LSST and DESI is an
important cross-check of the LSST cosmic shear results; we Ô¨Ånd that [they give consistent
constraints].

B.8.5.2. Science Objectives ‚ÄîTo perform this analysis, will require Rubin‚Äôs photo-z catalog
and shape catalog for the LSST sample. We will also need the DESI spectroscopic galaxy
catalog, of which there are already releases. The catalogs required for this joint analysis are
also necessary individually for each survey‚Äôs analysis, so this analysis would leverage the
existing work already performed to improve constraints at little additional cost.

Our analysis will be limited by the size of the survey overlap region; the choice of extending
the LSST footprint north further into the DESI footprint would allow for signiÔ¨Åcant increased
analysis power. To understand these limitations and make forecasts for the improvement
possible with this joint analysis, we could perform a precursor analysis with other data sets
of photometric sources and spectroscopic lenses such as KiDS and GAMA, or HSC and
SDSS-III/IV.

B.8.5.3. Challenges (what makes it hard) ‚ÄîThis joint analysis will require address a number
of challenges, including:

‚Ä¢ Understanding of clustering sample selection functions, but this should be handled
by DESI. Would result in catalogs of random points illustrating the coverage as a
DESI-provided data product.

‚Ä¢ We will rerun the analysis for each Rubin data release; the challenge is to make the

analysis easily re-runnable.

‚Ä¢ The photo-z quality for Y1-Y2 LSST data will be lower than Y10, and the early LSST
overlap with DESI may be less uniform in image quality (and the high airmass may
be prioritized by the scheduler). Essentially, the depth maps may be more complex
and have more structure early in the survey at the edge of the footprint.

Data to Software to Science

171

‚Ä¢ Most/all LSST source photo-z tomographic bins assume calibration with a DESI-like
(probably DESI) sample; may need to worry about covariance when also using DESI
for cosmology measurements.

B.8.5.4. Running on LSST Datasets (for the Ô¨Årst 2 years) ‚ÄîWe will require the following data
sets and pay attention to relevant considerations as follows:

‚Ä¢ Shape catalog: Metacal should be available from the DM pipeline, Metadetect may

be available (otherwise would be a value added catalog from DESC).

‚Ä¢ As the LSST/DESI overlap area is at higher airmass than the average LSST airmass,
we will have to recalibrate the images in this region. As this region is ~1/5 of the
5~2.2 times larger error bars. (Note

weak lensing shears, but we will not have to fully re-do the shape measurements.).
As g-g lensing is more forgivable in terms on calibration parameters, this may be
tolerable. So a subset of the image simulations may be suÔ¨Écient.

‚àö

‚Ä¢ Estimate of number of galaxies that can serve as source galaxies for DESI LRGs in
early LSST data: 70 million. We obtain this from considering that in early LSST data,
an estimated source galaxy number density is 10 arcmin‚àí2, of which approximately
half are above the LRG redshift (The LSST Dark Energy Science Collaboration et al.
2018). Given the expected 4000 deg2 overlap region, this gives approximately 70
million sources.

‚Ä¢ Photoz for the shape catalog: Tomographic sample selection based on Rubin-provided

B.8.5.5. Precursor data sets ‚Äî

‚Ä¢ For the spectroscopic galaxy sample, we will be able to use early DESI data as a

precursor. We could also use GAMA, a spectroscopic survey, or SDSS-III/IV.

‚Ä¢ For the galaxy shape data set, we could use the Ultraviolet Near- Infrared Optical

Northern Survey (UNIONS) survey, HSC, or KiDS.

B.8.5.6. Analysis WorkÔ¨Çow ‚ÄîWe lay out the following workÔ¨Çow as a possible route to
completing this analysis:

‚Ä¢ Perform an optimized tomographic binning of shape sample, likely using Rubin-

provided photo-z estimates.

‚Ä¢ Use DESI Emission-Line Galaxies (ELG)s & quasars for photo-z calibration.
‚Ä¢ Obtain the DESI LRG catalog for the lens sample.
‚Ä¢ Using the window functions of both surveys, create samples in the overlap region of

the two surveys.

‚Ä¢ Compute correlation functions (galaxy-galaxy lensing, as well as the auto-correlation)

on these samples.

172

‚Ä¢ Implement null tests. This might include, for example, checking that high-redshift

lenses with low-redshift sources produce no signal.

‚Ä¢ Estimate a covariance matrix using theory predictions or empirical estimators (jack-
knife etc.), accounting for correlations between the diÔ¨Äerent quantities being mea-
sured.

‚Ä¢ Carry out the cosmological parameter likelihood analysis given the data vectors,

covariances, and models for systematic errors.

B.8.5.7. Software Capabilities Needed ‚Äî

‚Ä¢ We will require software to compute the correlation functions, such as TreeCorr13

‚Ä¢ We will need to store these correlation functions as intermediate data products, but

this will not require a large amount of space.

B.8.6. Weak lensing cosmology analysis / 3x2pt

Contributors:
cois.lanussse@cea.fr)

Andresa Campos

(acampos@cmu.edu), Francois Lanusse (fran-

B.8.6.1. Abstract ‚ÄîWe present the Ô¨Årst cosmological analysis of the weak lensing shear-
shear correlation functions in combination with galaxy clustering and galaxy-galaxy lensing,
which trace the evolution of large-scale structure from intermediate to low redshift. With
the Ô¨Årst year of LSST data, we can already constrain the amplitude of matter Ô¨Çuctuations
to greater precision than precursor surveys such as KiDS, DES, and HSC, reaching ~1-%
precision when considering statistical and systematic uncertainty. This improved result is
due to the increased area of LSST, and the fact that the Ô¨Årst year of data is comparable
in depth to DES and deeper than KiDS. This work will rely on analysis of the catalogs
produced by the LSST Science Pipelines down to detections with ~10-sigma signiÔ¨Åcance.
This result is suÔ¨Éciently precise to permit a robust test of LCDM by comparing with the
amplitude from the CMB, which current analyses suggest may be higher than that from
weak lensing (implying some uncontrolled systematic or a failure of the LCDM model).
Together with CMB data, we can go beyond the LCDM model and provide competitive
constraints on the equation of state of dark energy.

B.8.6.2. Science Objectives ‚ÄîMilestones to this result include deÔ¨Åning samples that pass
basic null tests, validating that the shear estimates meet the needs for the science, and
estimating N(z) for the samples selected based on photo-z. For the last step, we do not
necessarily need very precise photo-z (though that would improve the localization of the
selected bins and improve the constraint on w) but we do need a very precise understanding
of the N(z) for the ensemble. At this stage, we anticipate that photo-z calibration and the
impact of blending on shear and photo-z are likely to be the key limited systematics.

B.8.6.3. Challenges (what makes it hard) ‚ÄîSystematics! This science case directly inherits
from the cosmic-shear systematics, we refer to Section B.8.1 for details, and here we
document the additional galaxy-clustering challenges.

‚Ä¢ Galaxy bias modeling: accurate modeling for galaxy bias used for galaxy-galaxy
and galaxy clustering modeling will be needed. Ongoing DESC bias challenge is
investigating various nonlinear bias models to reach Y1 and Y10 requirements.

‚Ä¢ Baryonic EÔ¨Äects: feedback from stars and supermassive black holes (AGN) redis-
tributes gas. The eÔ¨Äects of galaxy formation are degenerate with cosmology/ neutrino
eÔ¨Äects, therefore ignoring baryons at small scales leads to biases (see Schneider et al.
(2020)). It is necessary to model the baryonic eÔ¨Äect and marginalize of it.

‚Ä¢ Deprojection of systematics maps:

to clean potential contamination from varying
survey and observing conditions variability, a step of mode deprojection from a set
of systematics maps can be applied (see e.g. Alonso et al. (2019)).

‚Ä¢ Blinding: a blinding scheme that encompasses multiple probes is a challenge on its
on, as discussed in Muir et al. (2020). Added to that, there is the additional question on

174

how eÔ¨Äective the blinding of DESC data products will be, given that Rubin products
will be available.

B.8.6.4. Existing Tools ‚Äî

‚Ä¢ What tools exist today to undertake these analyses

‚Äì RAIL+qp for getting p(z) for each object
‚Äì Metadetection algorithm (descwl_coadd, mdet-shear-sims. . . )
‚Äì Code from Pedersen et al. on self-calibrating IA
‚Äì Synthetic Source Injection (DM/DESC)
‚Äì Systematics maps building (e.g. Supreme)
‚Äì Theoretical modelling (e.g., CCL, HMCode/HaloÔ¨Åt), correlation functions (e.g.,
TreeCorr, NaMaster, TXPipe), Covariances (TJPCosmo), Inference (e.g., Cos-
moSIS)

‚Ä¢ What functionality is missing from these tools and frameworks that would need to be
developed, or is there some issue with their application to the dataset at LSST scale?

‚Äì Toolset is fairly complete for Y1

‚Ä¢ If new tools were built what components from existing frameworks would be critical

to keep? (e.g. what parts of existing tools work well)

‚Ä¢ Data release catalogs for the WFD survey - we‚Äôll use essentially all galaxies down to

in ellipticity errorbars by more than a factor of 2 with LSST is combined with Euclid)

B.8.6.7. Analysis WorkÔ¨Çow ‚ÄîThis science case directly inherits from the cosmic-shear,
galaxy clustering and galaxy-galaxy lensing workÔ¨Çow. We refer to Section B.8.1 and
Section B.8.5 for details on each probe. Here we provide the general steps and document
the additional items related to the combined 3x2pt analyses:

‚Ä¢ Apply selection criteria to the source and lens catalogs.
‚Ä¢ Apply the optimal redshift estimation method to each catalog, perform tomography

and estimate the N(z) distribution.

‚Ä¢ Measure each of the three two-point correlations: cosmic shear, galaxy clustering

and galaxy-galaxy lensing.

‚Ä¢ Compute the joint covariance matrix of the three probes.
‚Ä¢ Model and mitigate systematics.
‚Ä¢ Perform likelihood analyses and cosmological parameter estimation.
‚Ä¢ Test for internal consistency of probes before unblinding.

B.8.6.8. Software Capabilities Needed ‚Äî

‚Ä¢ Survey property maps (e.g., skyproj w/ healsparse, Supreme)
‚Ä¢ Calexps and coadds
‚Ä¢ N(z)s estimator
‚Ä¢ Sampler

B.8.6.9. References ‚ÄîPrecursor survey analyses:

‚Ä¢ DES-Y3: Abbott et al. (2022)
‚Ä¢ KiDS-1000: Heymans et al. (2021)

C.2. Cross Matching

Contributors: Viviana Acquaviva, Igor Andreoni, Leanne Guy, Saavik Ford, Nico
Garavito-Camargo, Mario Juric (editor), Ilhuiyolitzin Villicana-Pedraza, Jeremy Kubica,
Samuel Wyatt, Weixiang Yu, Alex Riley

C.2.1. Abstract

A signiÔ¨Åcant fraction of science use cases presented at the Workshop require the ability
to (generally positionally) cross-correlate the detections in the LSST catalog with one or
more other catalogs ‚Äì an operation commonly known as ‚Äúcross-matching‚Äù. This capability
would enable enrichment of LSST data with information taken in other wavelengths, at
other times, diÔ¨Äerent resolutions, or of generally diÔ¨Äerent characteristics. This capability
is needed in two regimes: a) real-time ‚Äì low-latency matching of O(10k) sources to O(10)
catalogs each holding O(1Bn) objects (to support adding information to alert streams from
other catalogs), and b) oÔ¨Ñine processing ‚Äì the ability to match O(10Bn) x O(1Bn) object
catalogs, followed by joining data from both catalogs (e.g., full time series of observations,
multi-wavelength studies) for analysis at scale. This capability should be easy to use for
the end-user. For example, it may be provided at the community broker level for real-time
cases, or accessible as simple Python calls or SQL-like statements callable from Python
notebooks for the oÔ¨Ñine-level.

We note that cross-matching is just a Ô¨Årst step in the process, nearly always followed by
bringing in additional data from catalogs being cross-matched. It may be better to think
of it as (distributed) joining of (large) tables on a spatial (user-deÔ¨Åned) index, followed by
(potentially heavy) computation on the result of the join. The cross-match capability may
therefore need to be a part of a larger scalable analytics system.

Data to Software to Science

177

C.2.2. Science Cases Needing this Tool

Solar system:

‚Ä¢ Multifrequency study of the Solar System moons and hazardous asteroids (Sec-

tion B.7.4)

Local universe static

‚Ä¢ The properties of the faint end of the Main Sequence: the stellar/sub-stellar boundary.

(Section B.5.3)

‚Ä¢ Mapping the Accreted and Intrinsic Stellar Populations in the Milky Way (Sec-

tion B.5.1)

‚Ä¢ Local Group Dwarf Galaxies, bound and unbound (Section B.5.2)
‚Ä¢ The local IMF as inferred from nearby star forming regions and clusters (Sec-

tion B.5.4)

Local universe variable & transient:

‚Ä¢ All science-cases involving enriching discovered transients or variability with non-

LSST data.

Extragalactic static:

‚Ä¢ Estimation of galaxy physical parameters with SED Ô¨Åtting (Section B.2.4)

Extragalactic variable:

‚Ä¢ Find All the AGN ASAP (Section B.4.3)
‚Ä¢ Connection between short term variability of AGN and their long term behavior

(Section B.4.4)

‚Ä¢ Augmenting AGN variability (Section B.4.1)

Extragalactic Transient:

‚Ä¢ Real time transient host association
‚Ä¢ Immediate classiÔ¨Åcation of astrophysical transients (Section B.3.1)
‚Ä¢ Non-localized alert alert crossmatching

C.2.3. Requirements for the software

We identify two rough clusters of use-cases, with diÔ¨Äering scalability and performance

requirements:

1. Real-time cross-matching: The ability to return multiple (say, N<=10) neighbors
within ~5‚Äù, with latency at alert scale (seconds), and the numbers of objects being
cross-matched similar to the numbers of alerts. The system well need to cross-
match to multiple (arbitrary) catalogs with a choice of distance metrics (e.g., on-sky,
real-space). Brokers may enrich alerts w. this information, therefore needing close
collaboration with broker teams wishing to operate this functionality.

178

2. Large-scale static-sky cross-matching: The ability to do full catalog cross matches
for statistical analysis. Matching to a Ô¨Åltered subset will be an important feature
‚Äì for example, a query such as ‚Äúfor these 10M objects, Ô¨Ånd 3 nearest ùëî ‚àí ùëü < 0.5
neighbors‚Äù. This system would need to:

‚Ä¢ Scale to O(10Bn) x O(1Bn) catalog cross-matches and repeat on ~monthly

scales.

‚Ä¢ Allow matching as close to interactive as possible (e.g., with smaller catalogs)

to enable exploratory science.

‚Ä¢ Have the ability to do faster cross-matching on smaller subsamples of these

objects, for testing purposes.

Aspects that are common to both use cases include:

‚Ä¢ Cross-matching is just a Ô¨Årst step in the process, nearly always followed by bringing
in additional data from catalogs being cross-matched. It may be better to think of
this as (distributed) joining of (large) tables on a spatial (user-deÔ¨Åned) index ‚Äì a joint
table of 100M objects is not very useful if the data on those objects (e.g., time series)
can‚Äôt be easily pulled/fused/processed together.

‚Ä¢ Desirability of probabilistic cross-matching, which takes into account Ô¨Ånite extent or
a large uncertainty ellipse for some objects. This may be emulated by the user if a
basic cross-matching capability where N nearest neighbors are returned is provided
For example, the user can take N nearest cross matches, compute some probability
function that each of them is the ‚Äútrue‚Äù match, and pick the most probable one (or
keep the full distribution).

‚Ä¢ N-way cross-matching (cross matching to multiple catalogs)
‚Ä¢ Reporting non-detections is important. E.g., for Solar System, transients/variability,

etc. Related to (reliable) selection functions.

Challenges:

‚Ä¢ The low latency and partial scalability for the real-time (alerts) use case.
‚Ä¢ The scalability for the online case, where large (likely distributed) catalogs are being
joined. For example cross-matching the LSST (located at SLAC) and the DES catalog
(located at NCSA), joining the time-series, and passing them on to a Python function
to compute some metric of interest on the joined light curve.

‚Ä¢ The join predicate which may be more complex than simple spatial join (e.g., ‚ÄúÔ¨Ånd

N closest matches in a user-deÔ¨Åned subset of a catalog‚Äù).

C.2.4. Running on LSST and other Datasets

As discussed above, this component is expected to be needed both for real-time and batch

processing use cases.

C.2.5. Existing Tools

‚Ä¢ Codes such as the Apache Spark based AXS, for scalable distributed joining/cross-

matching of extremely large datasets.

Data to Software to Science

179

‚Ä¢ Codes such as catsHTM and similar for fast in-memory crossmatching
‚Ä¢ Online cross-match services like CDS XMatch and many others
‚Ä¢ HEALPix Alchemy for non-localized events such as GW/Neutrino Alerts. Has the
capability to crossmatch catalogs, observational footprints, and all-sky images within
a healpix map.

‚Ä¢ Cross-match services being developed as parts of community brokers

Needed enhancements:

‚Ä¢ Scalability ‚Äì many of these tools assume O(<1k-1M) object operation
‚Ä¢ Need arbitrary catalog cross-matching, w/o pre-computed join tables and with user-

deÔ¨Åned predicates

‚Ä¢ N-way cross-match ‚Äì the ability to join many catalogs
‚Ä¢ Distributed joins ‚Äì many of these tools focus on just computing cross-match, but
science use cases need to bring together and work on the data as the next steps (e.g.,
light curves, spectra, sometimes even images).

‚Ä¢ User-friendly distributed operation ‚Äì the user should be able to use these tools with
similar ease to using a RDBMS (Relational DataBase Management System) today.
For example, the user should be able to write a declarative statement about the result
they‚Äôre trying to achienve, and have the execution ‚Äì query optimization, scaling,
potential work distribution, fault tolerance ‚Äì be handled transparently.

C.2.6. Computational WorkÔ¨Çow

Computational workÔ¨Çows are somewhat science dependent, and many have been

discussed in speciÔ¨Åc science use cases. Here we just give two representative examples:

Example from the stellar/sub-stellar boundary science case:

‚Ä¢ Open a notebook and query some sources by making cuts (in color) on the object

catalog data to identify candidate low-mass stars.

‚Ä¢ Identify candidates with early proper motion and parallax measurements.
‚Ä¢ Get sources data (light curves) and/or take source measurements for each selected

object.

‚Ä¢ Run routines to identify variability.
‚Ä¢ Cross-match with calibration subsets (Gaia, spectroscopic surveys, etc.)

An example from the SED Ô¨Åtting science case:

‚Ä¢ Open a notebook and query some sources by making cuts for example in redshift or

luminosity.

‚Ä¢ Run some object classiÔ¨Åcation algorithm to identify which sources are galaxies.
‚Ä¢ Cross match those galaxies with sources from other multi-wavelength catalogs to

obtain a ‚Äúwider‚Äù SED.

‚Ä¢ Do science with the cross matched data (validation of LSST-results, SED Ô¨Åtting on

multi-wavelength SED, comparison with simulations, and others).

C.3. Selection Functions

Contributors: Yusra AlSayyad, Katelyn Breivik, Giulio Fabbian, Matt Holman, Adrian
Price-Whelan, Kate Storey-Fisher

C.3.1. Abstract

Selection functions are core components of any modeling procedure that aims to quantify
the population statistics or density distribution of sources or objects. A selection function
for a given modeling method may contain things like the detection eÔ¨Éciency of sources
with a given brightness or shape, the classiÔ¨Åcation accuracy of sources, the cadence of
observations, the Milky Way and intergalactic dust distribution, or the crowdedness (in
source counts) of a Ô¨Åeld. While the LSST Data Management (DM) group plans to provide
the core data products, the detection eÔ¨Éciency of a theoretical point source per position and
epoch and each coadd, each speciÔ¨Åc science case, classiÔ¨Åcation, or detection algorithm will
need a specialized selection function which depends on the science question or model being
studied. What we are therefore currently missing in the community are worked examples
of how to construct and use selection functions of varied complexity for diÔ¨Äerent use cases.

C.3.2. Science Cases Needing this Tool

Any science cases that want to learn population statistics or source density distributions.

C.3.3. Requirements for the software

We recommend producing 3‚Äì5 science demonstrations that utilize the DM data products
and metadata to construct selection functions that are used in illustrative science examples.
As possible example use cases, we recommend:

‚Ä¢ A selection function combining the depth of coadd images over the sky with a
dustmap, to evaluate a model for the spherically-averaged distribution of stars in the
Milky Way‚Äôs stellar halo.

‚Ä¢ Tools and worked examples on how to convert from the pixelized/rasterized repre-
sentation of detection maps that DM will provide to vector/polygon representations
for users who need them.

‚Ä¢ Worked examples for generating simulations either at the catalog-level or realized
images for injection into the Rubin images via the Pipelines‚Äô synthetic source injection
framework.

‚Ä¢ A selection function that incorporates cadence or time of observation information as
a function of sky position to assess the detectability of RR Lyrae stars to measure the
period and magnitude distributions of RR Lyrae stars. Similar for variability-selected
AGN populations.

‚Ä¢ A selection function for detecting asteroids of a given shape in single images to

compare the numbers of asteroids with diÔ¨Äerent shape characteristics.

182

C.3.4. Running on LSST and other Datasets

This requires LSST (coadd and single-epoch) image masks, property maps and both coadd
and time-series catalog information. SpeciÔ¨Åc tutorials will pull in case-speciÔ¨Åc info such
as dustmaps and variability models.

C.3.5. Existing Tools

The core data products are planned to be produced by the DM team, but what is missing

are demonstrations of how to construct selection functions of diÔ¨Äerent types/forms.

C.3.6. Computational WorkÔ¨Çow

n/a

C.4. Time Series

C.4.1. Parametric Fitting

Contributors: Catarina S. Alves (mailto:catarina.alves.18@ucl.ac.uk), Matthew Graham,
Andrew Bradshaw, Andrew Connolly, Garrett Levine, David Trilling, Fabio Ragosta, Tomas
Ahumada, Jing Lu, Alex Gagilano, Neven Caplar, Illƒ≥a Medan

C.4.1.1. Abstract ‚ÄîMany analyses involve Ô¨Åtting a prespeciÔ¨Åed model to data where the
model parameters have semantic content, for example, they represent physical quantities.
Given the numerous and diverse objects that Rubin LSST will observe with unprecedented
precision and time coverage, including solar system objects, stars of all stripes, transients,
and variable galaxy images, the models which are Ô¨Åt to the observations must similarly
be Ô¨Çexible while also including physical information about each object. Commonly used
tools in parametric time series analysis should be automatically computed (or trained) on all
objects on a regular basis, along with speciÔ¨Åc subsets being analyzed with targeted tools as
needed; all provided through a uniÔ¨Åed interface with a common data structure. Additionally,
model selection criteria such as AIC or Bayes factors shall also be pre-computed to enable
comparison between models, along with uncertainties and ranges of validity for model
parameters. Providing the data alongside informative statistics in a networked and uniÔ¨Åed
interface should help maximize the potential of LSST.

C.4.1.2. Science Cases Needing this Tool ‚ÄîParametric Ô¨Åtting to time series is common to
many areas of astronomy, including:

‚Ä¢ cosmology (in particular, SN Ia cosmology to produce distance estimates)
‚Ä¢ AGN to model stochastic variability
‚Ä¢ stars to model particular periodic structure in light curves, e.g., RR Lyrae
‚Ä¢ exoplanets to model background stellar activity

C.4.1.3. Requirements for the software ‚ÄîThe primary requirements for the software are that
it should:

‚Ä¢ scale eÔ¨Éciently to arbitrary data sizes
‚Ä¢ be Ô¨Çexible across diÔ¨Äerent model structures and Ô¨Åtting constraints, e.g., optimizer

choice, loss functions

‚Ä¢ be able to provide uncertainties (posterior distributions) on both model parameters

and predicted values
‚Ä¢ run as fast as possible

In most cases, the input will be time series, either individual or batches, and these will
be irregularly and sparsely sampled, gappy, and heteroskedastic. The time series may also
be univariate (single Ô¨Ålter) or multivariate (ugrizy). It may be that this is best supported
through a common time series data model.

184

A lot of parameter Ô¨Åtting codes exist for speciÔ¨Åc science cases (see below) so rather
than implementing new versions of these, the software requirements is to run these in an
appropriate environment. ModiÔ¨Åcation of existing codes might be needed to implement the
uncertainty requirement and to improve the speed and scalability of the code.

C.4.1.4. Running on LSST and other Datasets ‚ÄîThis technical case will use time-series, whose
needed duration depends on the speciÔ¨Åc use case. The datasets could come both from
calibrated images and data release catalogs. The alert stream data may present challenegs
for parametric Ô¨Åts because the alerts use diÔ¨Äerent templates across the years, which could
result in artiÔ¨Åcial discontinuities.

SN Ia cosmology is a science case associated with parametric Ô¨Åts. There are numerous
precursor datasets from previous surveys. More recently, we have data from the Zwicky
Transient Facility (ZTF) and LSST-like simulated datasets such as the Photometric LSST
Astronomical Time-series ClassiÔ¨Åcation Challenge (PLAsTiCC; The PLAsTiCC team et al.
2018; Kessler et al. 2019) and its update, ELAsTiCC. The analysis of these precursor datasets
will lead to publications, in particular when applied to real data. A comparison of template
Ô¨Åtting algorithms on the same datasets, including the advantages and limitations of each
methodology, would also lead to a new publication.

C.4.1.5. Existing Tools ‚Äî

‚Ä¢ Tools (SN Ia light curve Ô¨Åtting + SED templates + AGN): SALT2 (Guy et al. 2007;
Taylor et al. 2021), SALT3 (Kenworthy et al. 2021), SUGAR (L√©get et al. 2020),
ParSNIP (Boone 2021), SNooPy (Burns et al. 2010), BayeSED (Han & Han 2012,
2014, 2018); EzTao (Yu & Richards 2022)

‚Ä¢ There is no easy way to compare between the above tools. Moreover, the SALT light

curve Ô¨Åtters are too slow for LSST use.

‚Ä¢ It is crucial to work with the community to understand how to implement the uniÔ¨Åed
interface with a common data structure to maintain the ease to use of the existing
tools.

C.4.1.6. Computational WorkÔ¨Çow ‚ÄîPick your optimizer, pick your loss function. Compress
data from photometry as a function of time into phase and amplitude.

‚Ä¢ SALT2 (Guy et al. 2007; Taylor et al. 2021)

‚Ä¢ SALT3 https://saltshaker.readthedocs.io/ (Kenworthy et al. 2021)

‚Ä¢ SUGAR (L√©get et al. 2020)

‚Ä¢ ParSNIP (Boone 2021)

C.4.2. Tools to Facilitate Anomaly Detection and Characterization in LSST Time-Series Data

Contributors: Alex Gagliano (gaglian2@illinois.edu), W. Garrett Levine, Neven Caplar,
Ashish Mahabal, Catarina S. Alves, Matthew Graham, Andrew Bradshaw, Andrew Con-
nolly, David Trilling, Fabio Ragosta, Tomas Ahumada, Jing Lu, Ilƒ≥a Medan

C.4.2.1. Abstract ‚ÄîRubin will generate time series photometry for tens of billions of
sources in multiple Ô¨Ålters to unprecedented depth over its ten-year survey. These data will
contain a dizzying breadth of persistent variable and non-variable phenomena, and some
of these will be observed for the Ô¨Årst time. Here we primarily concentrate on the data
released through LSST alerts and the anomalies lurking in the alert stream. To optimize
the use of the Rubin Observatory as a discovery machine for rare and high-priority events,
infrastructure must be developed to eÔ¨Éciently identify anomalies among massive datasets
in a timely manner. This will require synergy between state-of-the-art machine learning
tools for anomaly detection and visualization techniques for interactive and low-latency
high-level analysis. These proposed tools should be suÔ¨Éciently scalable and fast enough
to enable prioritization and follow-up of rapidly-evolving events before they dim (such as,
early SN interaction, cometary outbursts or breakup, rapidly changing AGN, microlensing
events/TDEs/kilonovae/other unknown phenomena and extreme cases of known types).

C.4.2.2. Science Cases Needing this Tool ‚ÄîDetecting anomalies in the alert stream will be
essential for identifying:

‚Ä¢ Comets experiencing volatile outbursts or breakup
‚Ä¢ Intrinsically-rare known classes of transients (kilonovae/TDEs/FBOTs)
‚Ä¢ Mapping SMBH Near Fields with Microlensing
‚Ä¢ Connection between short term variability of AGN and their long term behavior
‚Ä¢ Extreme cases of known types of variables

In addition to elucidating the physics powering known signals, anomalies will also represent
entirely new classes of phenomena probing poorly-explored regimes in event brightness and
timescale. The science enabled by this infrastructure cannot be fully known in advance but
will have signiÔ¨Åcant impact, ensuring the legacy of LSST as a pioneer in transient discovery
for decades to come.

C.4.2.3. Requirements for the software ‚ÄîAnomalies in the event stream will manifest them-
selves in diÔ¨Äerence images and require low latency access. Because of the value of rapid
follow-up, anomaly detection algorithms must keep pace with the alert stream and should be
able to operate on as few bits of information as possible. Provided that anomaly detection
is suÔ¨Éciently fast and high-level processing is done by Rubin, the continuous alert stream
could include a Ô¨Çag speciÔ¨Åcally for these events. Deriving meaningful information from
anomalies will require Ô¨Çexible frameworks that can be re-trained and improved with rapid
feedback processes, as many of the events we aim to discover will be ‚Äúunknown unknowns‚Äù
before Rubin comes online. Software that can accurately characterize astronomical events

Data to Software to Science

187

in real-time remain woefully absent from the literature, and this presents a barrier to survey
readiness.

The survey‚Äôs sensitivity to unusual variable phenomena is limited by the template gen-
eration and subsequent image diÔ¨Äerencing conducted by the survey. Where possible, the
template images and strategy for generating them should be reported, and the raw images
should be provided through the data releases in case users wish to construct custom tem-
plates for their science case. The Rubin Science Platform could also allow for users to
construct custom diÔ¨Äerence imaging pipelines close to the data to avoid duplication of data.
While high-redshift events will be contained to small postage stamps, active Solar System
objects will require on-demand custom image cutouts (in some cases, as large as 10‚Äôx10‚Äô)
for full characterization. Even for stationary events, contextual information such as that
provided by the host galaxy of an explosive transient is valuable for further characterizing
a source at early epochs. Events that are not identiÔ¨Åed through the alert stream or for which
larger cutouts are needed could be retrieved through the data releases, although this 24-hour
latency would present an additional obstacle to rapid discovery. To manage bandwidth, a
queue system or resource allocation framework among the scientiÔ¨Åc stakeholders could be
implemented.

C.4.2.4. Running on LSST and other Datasets ‚ÄîAnomaly detection will leverage the alert
stream and its associated postage stamps. Algorithms to Ô¨Ånd anomalies are expected to
function more eÔ¨Äectively with long baselines, but it would be ideal to identify anomalies
in short light curves. This use case would be especially relevant for newly discovered Solar
System small bodies approaching perihelia. Data releases for anomalous but persistent
variable sources would be valuable for archival studies of these populations. Finally, rapid
catalog cross-matching will be useful to determine the multi-wavelength properties of
LSST-discovered sources and further investigate their underlying physics.

It is essential to identify the relevant software systems and train them with simulated
samples in advance of LSST Ô¨Årst light. On the hunt for extra-galactic transients spanning a
broad range in progenitor physics, the data and models produced by the Photometric LSST
Astronomical Time-Series ClassiÔ¨Åcation Challenge (PLAsTiCC) challenge have been state-
of-the-art. These models have been improved (and the data made more realistic) in the
Extended LSST Astronomical Time-Series ClassiÔ¨Åcation Challenge (ELAsTiCC), but data
are still limited to the classes for which realistic rest-frame SED models are available.

C.4.2.5. Existing Tools ‚ÄîCentral to the question of anomalous behavior is the ‚Äúdistance"
between an event in question and a larger population. An event with a large distance from the
population is considered anomalous. These distance measures could be calculated in data
space (comparing light curves between events), parametric feature space (characterizing
e.g., the period and amplitude of each light curve and comparing these), or non-parametric
feature space (reduced-dimensionality embeddings of the original data). To generate fea-
ture representations of ingested data, methods often require close integration with feature
extraction (period-Ô¨Ånding) and dimensionality reduction tools (tSNE, UMAP, Principal

188

Component Analysis (PCA)). These tools can also be used to provide high-level summaries
of large datasets, with which the astronomer may be able to identify anomalies by eye.

Methods diÔ¨Äer in their focus on characterizing anomalies directly, or indirectly by con-
straining the properties of the ‚Äúnormal‚Äù larger population. The latter is more common,
although novel methods are in active development.
Isolation forests are an example of
direct anomaly identiÔ¨Åcation, and they are used by SNAD (Malanchev et al. 2021) to iden-
tify unusual supernovae and S√°nchez-S√°ez et al. (2021) to Ô¨Ånd unusual AGN (in the AGN
case, a variational autoencoder is Ô¨Årst used to generate a set of non-parametric features). A
wealth of machine learning tools, however, focus on the bulk properties of the population
(which can inform objects at the boundaries of classiÔ¨Åcation), and the vast majority of these
rely on decision trees (including boosted decision trees and random forests) for classifying
common events. An event with low classiÔ¨Åcation probability reported by these methods
may be an anomaly.

A non-exhaustive list of open-source codes for photometric classiÔ¨Åcation of supernovae

include:

‚Ä¢ Avocado: A gradient-boosted decision tree classiÔ¨Åer for anomaly detection (Boone

2019).

‚Ä¢ ParSNIP: A deep neural network that calculates latent parameters of light curves with

an encoder (Boone 2021).

‚Ä¢ RAPID: A recurrent neural network for real-time classiÔ¨Åcation (Muthukrishna et al.

2019).

‚Ä¢ SuperRAENN: A recurrent autoencoder coupled to a random forest algorithm for

classiÔ¨Åcation (Villar et al. 2020).

‚Ä¢ snmachine: A framework for light curve feature extraction and classiÔ¨Åcation using

multiple techniques (Lochner et al. 2016; Alves et al. 2022).

‚Ä¢ superNNova: A Bayesian recurrent neural network for real-time classiÔ¨Åcation (M√∂ller

& de Boissi√®re 2019).

Density-based methods are also in development (e.g., DBscan14; Local outlier factor15).

Because anomalies are deÔ¨Åned relative to a population, there must also exist an interface
near the data (ideally embedded within the Rubin Science Platform) for rapidly interacting
with a large sample of alerts. A gap exists in current tools for early/real-time classiÔ¨Åcation,
and this needs to be resolved prior to LSST Ô¨Årst light as it is central to enabling follow-up
before an anomaly has ended. Paths to resolving major current barriers include:

‚Ä¢ Introducing a broad diversity of unknown signals, parameterized by phenomenol-
ogy, into simulated data spanning multiple orders of magnitudes in brightness and
timescale.

14 https://scikit-learn.org/stable/modules/generated/sklearn.cluster.DBSCAN.html
15 https://scikit-learn.org/stable/auto_examples/neighbors/plot_lof_outlier_detection.html

Data to Software to Science

189

‚Ä¢ Training and validating softwares that can rapidly recover these signals from simulated
data streams with noisy and incomplete data (a few e.g., <5 photometric points and
small postage stamps).

‚Ä¢ Building tools that are highly scalable to millions of objects with runtimes of ~minutes

or less

‚Ä¢ Constructing software that is adaptable to new datasets and rapid re-training (active

learning)

In the direction of Ô¨Çexible detection methods, Astronomaly (Lochner & Bassett 2021) has
recently proposed a general framework in which active learning is used to leverage user-
identiÔ¨Åed outliers and inform automated anomaly detection. Active learning in astronomy
remains underdeveloped, but will likely play a powerful role in facilitating rapid anomaly
identiÔ¨Åcation and follow-up in the Ô¨Årst few years of LSST.

C.4.2.6. Computational WorkÔ¨Çow ‚ÄîThe raw alert stream data (postage stamps and imme-
diate photometry) will be accessed through community alert brokers.The brokers and/or
Rubin Science Platform should provide well-calibrated historical light curves (including
non-detections via forced photometry) across all diaSources at the location of the alert.
Photometry for Solar System objects, which will be moving relative to the background
stars, will need to track the objects themselves.

Real-time anomaly detection with LSST will likely be conducted as follows. Anomaly
detection algorithms should Ô¨Årst rapidly analyze newly acquired photometry within the
context of prior light curve data. Global features such as period, parametric Ô¨Åt parameters,
and dimensionality-reduction components should then be calculated for each event. Local
features computed across windowed components of a light curve (e.g., outbursts, changing
states) should also be computed to help identify anomalous behavior within an otherwise
common event.

In addition, anomaly identiÔ¨Åcation routines must qualitatively and quantitatively estimate
the similarity between global and local features of single events and for these events in
comparison to all events across the LSST catalog. This could occur via machine-learning
tools to Ô¨Çag high-distance objects/phenomena (the quantitative approach), or visualization
tools for assessing the spread between these scales (the qualitative approach). Ideally, this
work will be done in parallel.

Finally, the anomaly detection infrastructure should be integrated into the LSST moni-
toring routines for individual working groups. These groups will need to query enlarged
postage stamps and acquire high cadence Target of Opportunity photometry for extremely
high-value objects. Supernovae will also need to be classiÔ¨Åed via spectroscopic follow-up.
